  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer7946095301657150459"><b>
  <i>Librarian Note: If you're looking for the Audible adaptation of the first three volumes <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53406893-the-sandman' rel='nofollow noopener'>it is here.</a></i>
</b><br/><br/>New York Times best-selling author Neil Gaiman's transcendent series SANDMAN is often hailed as the definitive Vertigo title and one of the finest achievements in graphic storytelling. Gaiman created an unforgettable tale of the forces that exist beyond life and death by </span>
  <span id="freeText7946095301657150459" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Librarian Note: If you're looking for the Audible adaptation of the first three volumes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53406893-the-sandman" rel="nofollow noopener">it is here.</a></i>
</b><br /><br />New York Times best-selling author Neil Gaiman's transcendent series SANDMAN is often hailed as the definitive Vertigo title and one of the finest achievements in graphic storytelling. Gaiman created an unforgettable tale of the forces that exist beyond life and death by weaving ancient mythology, folklore and fairy tales with his own distinct narrative vision.<br /><br />In PRELUDES &amp; NOCTURNES, an occultist attempting to capture Death to bargain for eternal life traps her younger brother Dream instead. After his 70 year imprisonment and eventual escape, Dream, also known as Morpheus, goes on a quest for his lost objects of power. On his arduous journey, Morpheus encounters Lucifer, John Constantine, and an all-powerful madman.<br /><br />This book also includes the story "The Sound of Her Wings," which introduces us to the pragmatic and perky goth girl Death.<br /><br />Includes issues 1-8 of the original series.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/916491-i-know-this-may-be-a-silly-question-but">I know this may be a silly question, but can some of the images be a little creepy or scary? I have read some of Gaimans other work and some of it was really weird. It Sandman a weird or somewhat scary book?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_916491'><a id="like_count_community_question_916491" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/916491?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F23754.Preludes_Nocturnes">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>

</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/54800223-hellen"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1595896324i/54800223._UX100_CR0,11,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/54800223-hellen">Hellen</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Some of the images can be disturbing, yes. There's a man exploding (imploding?), a woman's head popping off, at some point there's gooey flesh coverin<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Some of the images can be disturbing, yes. There's a man exploding (imploding?), a woman's head popping off, at some point there's gooey flesh covering the walls of a room... It gets less creepy later in the series, though, so don't let it stop you! Sandman is dreamy, fantastical and definitely weird at times, but (for me at least) it's not scary.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_2577446365" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2577446365" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2577446365?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span> (Review from the author)
          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/42409735-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=graphic-novels-you-should-read">graphic-novels-you-should-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-would-blurb">books-i-would-blurb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2577446365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15600774753662662211">So here's the thing...<br/><br/>I wrote the introduction to the 30th anniversary edition of The Sandman.<br/><br/>How absolutely *stupid* is that?<br/><br/>It's been half a year since I got the invitation, and months since I actually finished writing the introduction, but I still can't believe it.<br/><br/>To celebrate, I thought I'd write a review here. But rather than just my usual messy gush about how I love some story, I got permission from the publisher to re-print part of my introduction I wrote.<br/><br/>Share and enjoy...<br/><br/>**(What follows</span>
  <span id="freeText15600774753662662211" style="display:none">So here's the thing...<br /><br />I wrote the introduction to the 30th anniversary edition of The Sandman.<br /><br />How absolutely *stupid* is that?<br /><br />It's been half a year since I got the invitation, and months since I actually finished writing the introduction, but I still can't believe it.<br /><br />To celebrate, I thought I'd write a review here. But rather than just my usual messy gush about how I love some story, I got permission from the publisher to re-print part of my introduction I wrote.<br /><br />Share and enjoy...<br /><br />**(What follows is an excerpt from the intro)**<br /><br />I’ll admit, I’m at a bit of a loss as to what to do here.<br /><br />If you’ve already read Sandman, what can I tell you that you don’t already know deep in the secret corners of your heart? You know this story is lovely and brilliant and sweet and strange. You know it is beautiful and deep and wry and wondrous. You know.<br /><br />If you’ve already read this book, you know nothing I can say is as good as what waits for you ahead. <br /><br />So go. Stop reading this and go. <br /><br />If you haven’t read this book, and are, perhaps, standing in a bookstore or a comic shop, wondering if it’s worth your time, what I can say to convince you? Should I wax rhapsodic? Get lyrical and grandiose? Reference some of the story’s funnier jokes so I seem more clever than I really am? <br /><br />No. I love this book too much. I don’t want to spoil its secrets or steal its thunder. <br /><br />So let me tell you the simple truth. No hyperbole. <br /><br />Sandman changed my life. <br /><br />It’s not often you get to say that and mean it. But it’s true.<br /><br />If that’s not enough to convince you… I guess all that’s left is for me to tell you a story or two. Because that’s what I do. <br /><br />Stories are important, after all….<br /><br />* * *<br /><br />I came to comics late in life. I can’t tell you why. I was a voracious reader as a kid, going through pretty much every picture book in the local library until I finally started chapter books around age 9. Then I read a novel or two a day until I finished high school. <br /><br />Even as I slouched through college, comics simply weren’t on my radar. Didn’t occur to me to read them. Didn’t occur to me they might be worth reading. I had a couple thousand fantasy and sci-fi novels under my belt, and my classes were exposing me to Shakespeare and Chaucer, Sanskrit theater and the Harlem Renaissance poets. I read Roethke and Frost and Brooks and Baldwin. <br /><br />But comics? That was like… Garfield, right? And superheroes? I didn’t spare any thought for them, and when I did, I assumed they were (and I’m ashamed to write this now) silly bullshit for kids.<br /><br />I was well into my 20’s when, at a weekend-long party, I sat down in a quiet corner and idly picked up a copy of Dark Night Returns. I read the whole thing straight through, completely lost in it, deaf to the riot and welter around me. <br /><br />Hours later, I hunted down the person who had brought the book. I shook it at them, angry and incredulous, demanding: “Is it all as good as this?” <br /><br />“Oh no,” he said sadly. “But some of it is close.” <br /><br />First he gave we Watchmen, and it floored me despite the fact that I didn’t I didn’t know superheroes mythology from a hole in the ground.<br /><br />Next came Sandman. And it was unlike any story I’d ever read. In any genre. In any medium. I remember thinking, “Can you do this? Can you have Odin and angels and faeries and witches and… just everything? All at once? In the same story? Is this allowed?”<br /><br />It lit me up inside. I wouldn’t shut up about it. I’d give it to people and say, “You have to read this! It’s like Shakespeare!” <br /><br />I blush a little now, remembering that. It’s not the best comparison. It’s just that back then, Shakespeare was the best thing I’d ever read. <br /><br />The truth is, Shakespeare wishes he wrote something this good. <br /><br />* * *<br /><br />But let’s back up a bit. I’d prefer to be fully honest here. I didn’t feel that way about Sandman immediately. Not right out of the gate. <br /><br />I read this first graphic novel and liked it well enough. Preludes and Nocturnes is lovely. It introduces the world, the characters, there’s a nice little plot. Tension. Mystery. Hero’s Journey. Mythic underpinning. Decent to the underworld. Vengeance. Recovery of self. Got my RDA of all manner of awesome here. Cool. <br /><br />Then I kept reading, and the storytelling got looser. But I was still happy. Shakespeare shows up. I dig that. And there’s faerie tales. And… What? Are we in Africa now? Wait, is someone telling a story about a story inside a story? Okay. That’s cool. I guess this series is more like a bunch of different stories? But they’re all interesting, so who really cares if they don’t really have much to do with each other… <br /><br />Then I kept reading and there was a little plotline. And a new character or two. And… and… hold on. Wait. Does all this fit together? Has it all fit together from the beginning? <br /><br />Has everything been leading to an ending? <br /><br />Oh. Oh lord. I never knew a story could be like this. <br /><br />**(End excerpt)**<br /><br />There's more, of course. I do tend to go on a bit when it comes to books I love. But I'm guessing you get the gist of it. <br /><br />If you read The Sandman back in the day, trust me, it's as good as you remember.<br /><br />If you've never read The Sandman, this is the place to start. And trust me, it's absolutely worth your time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15600774753662662211" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_827069015" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/827069015" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/827069015?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-would-blurb">books-i-would-blurb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=graphic-novels-you-should-read">graphic-novels-you-should-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer827069015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13905018631650829256">First off, let's be clear, I'm a big soppy fan of Gaiman's Work. <br/><br/>Secondly, I've read the Sandman series several times before. So this is more a record of my fond re-reading and musing about this story rather than some sort of objective review. <br/><br/>But if you've read any of my other reviews, that won't come as a surprise. <br/><br/>I think it's been a full ten years since the last time I've read this series. And, given that my memory is, well... to put it plainly quite shit, I get to experience the series with</span>
  <span id="freeText13905018631650829256" style="display:none">First off, let's be clear, I'm a big soppy fan of Gaiman's Work. <br /><br />Secondly, I've read the Sandman series several times before. So this is more a record of my fond re-reading and musing about this story rather than some sort of objective review. <br /><br />But if you've read any of my other reviews, that won't come as a surprise. <br /><br />I think it's been a full ten years since the last time I've read this series. And, given that my memory is, well... to put it plainly quite shit, I get to experience the series with a fairly fresh set of eyes at this point. <br /><br />The first thing that I notice is that the art is different that I remember. I remember it being darker and grittier. More real AND more surreal at the same time. <br /><br />But that, I'm expecting, is the lens of corrective memory at play. In reality, the art seems more (for lack of a better word) cartoony. The colors are bolder, more garish than I remember. <br /><br />Note: I had the same experience recently when I re-read Watchmen. So I'm suspecting this is an issue with me, rather than the comic. Or perhaps I'm remembering the later parts of the comic more vividly. <br /><br />The other thing I'm surprised to see is that both John Constantine and a few members of the DC cannon show up in this first book. Mostly just brief walk-ons, but Martian Manhunter is there, and we see Arkham asylum and the Scarecrow. <br /><br />These things don't detract from the story. I can say that for a fact, because on my earlier reads when I hadn't read many comics, so I didn't know who many of those characters were. But I have to say, reading through this time they kinda surprised me. <br /><br />You see, I think of the Sandman series as being... well... kinda self-contained. That's not the right way of putting it really, as the story is hugely vast. It's sprawling and lovely and mythically rich. <br /><br />I guess what I'm saying here is that while it seems perfectly natural that Sandman exists in the same world as Odin and Puck and Eurydice, having Batman in that cosmology, even by association, feels really odd to me. And the more I think of it, it's strangely off-putting. <br /><br />That said, I'm fairly certain these DC cameos were early things, and weren't repeated extensively throughout the rest of the series. <br /><br />As for this first book, it makes for both a great story in itself, and a great start to the larger series. That's not a common thing. <br /><br />Is it worth your time?<br /><br />Absolutely. <br /><br />I don't care if you don't read comics, you should read this series. <br /><br />And if you *do* read comics, you cannot consider yourself well-read until you have Sandman under your belt. Seriously. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13905018631650829256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1645189528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1645189528" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1645189528?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1645189528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2052784093782210746"><br/>I thought it was time I added <i>The Sandman</i>. It fits precisely into my systematic program of reading. “Hold on!” I can hear all you folks out there saying. “ Your 'systematic program of reading'? Why you're one of the most eclectic readers I know!”<br/><br/>I can understand why you might feel that way, but there is a little method to my madness. Although it is true that some of the books I read (progressive political analyzes, slim 20th century verse collections, cat books, classic spiritual treatises, or t</span>
  <span id="freeText2052784093782210746" style="display:none"><br />I thought it was time I added <i>The Sandman</i>. It fits precisely into my systematic program of reading. “Hold on!” I can hear all you folks out there saying. “ Your 'systematic program of reading'? Why you're one of the most eclectic readers I know!”<br /><br />I can understand why you might feel that way, but there is a little method to my madness. Although it is true that some of the books I read (progressive political analyzes, slim 20th century verse collections, cat books, classic spiritual treatises, or the occasional literary novel) do not fit within my grand plan, most of them do.<br /><br />About ten years ago, I realized that many books I liked were from emerging, belittled or despised forms or genres: the Elizabethan popular stage play, the budding 18th century novel, the gothic romance, the Victorian <i>bourgeois</i> novel published in serial form, the novel of sensation, the decadent experiment, the adventure tale, the sea story, the spy story, the ghost story, the pulps (both mystery and terror), the space opera and other science fiction and fantasy of the '50's, the detective novel, and the modern horror tale. Whereas the writers of “literary fiction” are often too concerned with critical opinion, judiciously curbing their personal obsessions and producing unreadable “writerly” prose, the humble genre writer is more likely to give his “inner crazy” free rein, and either tell his eccentric story in no-nonsense fashion or--on occasion--let his plots and prose burgeon untrammeled, like a blooming meadow garden. At worst, the result is amusing, or odd; at best, it is something original, something new.<br /><br />Not long ago, it occurred to me that—being too much a creature of words—I had neglected an obvious example of an emerging genre: the comic book of the late 80's, on its way to becoming the “graphic novel.” Since I'm already in love with Gaiman's <i>American Gods</i>, I decided to begin with <i>The Sandman</i>.<br /><br />I am glad I did. The first eight numbers of Gaiman's series are, on the surface, a traditional quest story. Morpheus Lord of the Dreamworld, imprisoned for years by a sorcerer, goes searching for the objects of power—his helmet, his jewel, his pouch of dream-dust—so that he may once again exercise full power over his realm. But the stories it tells, and the genres in touches, range widely, from an Edwardian tale of an Aleister Crowley type sorcerer, a pulp EC Horror Cain and Abel anecdote, a contemporary British horror story of drug abuse, through a mythic descent into hell featuring an epic duel with demons, until it climaxes in a ghastly ordeal of modern horror (with a little meta-fiction thrown in) in which an escaped madman in a diner, in possession of the Dream Lord's mind manipulating jewel, brutalizes the restaurant's clientele for hours. The final number (“The Sound of Her Wings”)—perhaps the most elegant of the eight—is however, a kind of whimsical coda, introducing us to Dream's sister, the irreverent and down-to-earth Death.<br /><br />I thought everything about this saga—story, dialogue, art—was well executed...except for <i>one</i> small detail. Gaiman chooses to make a reference to the DC universe (the Justice League of America, to be precise) in a way that breaks the spell of what otherwise would be a completely self-contained, transcendent and mythic journey. But that's the kind of thing that's bound to happen in emerging genres. People take risks.<br /><br />Anyway, I'm hooked. On to <i>Sandman, Volume 2: “The Doll's House”</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2052784093782210746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1153614449?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=darkness-horror-gothic">darkness-horror-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=comic-graphic-novel">comic-graphic-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1153614449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13702114447055768900">Mr Gaiman, thank you for the dream. <br/><br/> See what I did there? But in all fairness this is a dream. This comic is just everything I want a comic to be. It’s dark and brutal; it’s profound and sophisticated: it’s imaginative and creative. I just love everything about it. It’s superb. Let’s begin with the characters:<br/><br/><u>
  <b>Dream</b>
</u><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471693275i/20092649._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='500' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>So the protagonist is a god, a very old god. And he represents one of the key elements of life itself: hope. He is everything the mind longs for. He is the reason to carry on livi</span>
  <span id="freeText13702114447055768900" style="display:none">Mr Gaiman, thank you for the dream. <br><br> See what I did there? But in all fairness this is a dream. This comic is just everything I want a comic to be. It’s dark and brutal; it’s profound and sophisticated: it’s imaginative and creative. I just love everything about it. It’s superb. Let’s begin with the characters:<br><br><u>
  <b>Dream</b>
</u><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471693275i/20092649._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So the protagonist is a god, a very old god. And he represents one of the key elements of life itself: hope. He is everything the mind longs for. He is the reason to carry on living. Dream walks in the darkness. He comes from the darkness and brings the light. However, such an entity has been brought down by the folly of humans. He’s been locked away and his power sought after. The world is in turmoil; thus, the story begins. As Dream seeks the stolen relics of his power, the depth of this world is slowly revealed. <br><br><u>
  <b>Lucifer Morningstar</b>
</u><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471693275i/20092650._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Although Lucifer isn’t a major character in this volume, I do feel like he is being forshadowed as a later antagonist in this series. Well, at least, I hope he is. I love the rendition of Lucifer here. He is very much in the Miltonic vein. Proud and beautiful yet twisted beyond repair. One of his minions has a relic of Dream’s, and although the two are hospitable towards each other, the words speak of veiled threats and a violent past. I can’t wait to see more. Lucifer and Dream philosophising the sovereignty of hell? Give me more. <br><br><u>
  <b>Death</b>
</u><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471693275i/20092651._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So Death is a gothic girl who oozes with nihilism? She’s a rock chick who is clearly rather badass. She’s Dream’s sister and is rather pissed off that he didn’t call on her for help. Why are these characters so fucking cool? I could spend all day reading about this lot. <br><br>Did I just find a new favourite comic series?<br></span>

<div id="review_644195878" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/644195878" />
    <a title="Alejandro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328672389p2/665584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/644195878?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alejandro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alejandro" href="/user/show/665584-alejandro">Alejandro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=comic-book">comic-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer644195878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8311598561522990020"><b>
  <u>FanTABulous preluDe!</u>
</b><br/><br/><br/><u>Creative Team:</u><br/><br/>Writer: Neil Gaiman<br/><br/>Illustrators: Sam Kieth, Mike Dringenberg & Malcolm Jones III<br/><br/>Covers: Dave McKean<br/><br/>Letterer: Todd Klein<br/><br/><br/><b>
  <u>A PRELUdE TO dREAM</u>
</b><br/><br/><b>
  <i>It is NEVER “only a dream”, John Constantine.</i>
</b><br/><br/>Definitely there is stuff that if you read again, you’ll get it differently, but even so, you’ll get it differently if you re-read it again in a different order.<br/><br/>I am re-reading again the first four TPBs of <i>The Sandman</i> since I was able to buy the rest of TPBs of the series</span>
  <span id="freeText8311598561522990020" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>FanTABulous preluDe!</u>
</b><br /><br /><br /><u>Creative Team:</u><br /><br />Writer: Neil Gaiman<br /><br />Illustrators: Sam Kieth, Mike Dringenberg &amp; Malcolm Jones III<br /><br />Covers: Dave McKean<br /><br />Letterer: Todd Klein<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>A PRELUdE TO dREAM</u>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>It is NEVER “only a dream”, John Constantine.</i>
</b><br /><br />Definitely there is stuff that if you read again, you’ll get it differently, but even so, you’ll get it differently if you re-read it again in a different order.<br /><br />I am re-reading again the first four TPBs of <i>The Sandman</i> since I was able to buy the rest of TPBs of the series, <b>BUT</b> I chose to read first the TPBs of <i>Death</i> and definitely it was the right call. Neil Gaiman commented at the end of <i>The High Cost of Living</i> that Death can serve as an introduction to <i>The Sandman</i> and he was right. I guess he knows something about it since he’s the author (he,he,he).<br /> <br />But, seriously, now that I read <i>Death: The High Cost of Living</i> first of re-engaging into <i>The Sandman</i>, I was able to recognize some characters and getting the importance of comments made there. Even wondering details like how Roderick Burguess was sure, with just a glance, that Dream wasn’t Death, but wasn’t able to know who was exactly. Allowing me to enjoy even more the re-reading experience of this first TPB in the series.<br /><br />Of course, re-reading again this first TPB (taking in account that I already read before the following 3 TPBs) allow me to enjoy the introduction here and there of characters that they will play relevant roles in the rest of the series. And that I was able to appreciate and understanding certain scenes.<br />So, indeed this is a prelude to <i>The Sandman</i>, since Neil Gaiman is just establishing the general scenario where he will develop the story in the following TPBs.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>BROKEN dREAM</u>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>I am riding in your dreams.</i>
</b><br /><br />1916: Morpheus, the King of the realm of Dreams, the embodiment of Dream, is trapped by mistake by a black sorceress.<br /><br />Roderick Burguess didn’t want to catch Dream, he was after Death.<br /><br />But what’s done, it’s done.<br /><br />Dream is trapped in a magic circle during 72 years and even bereft from his tools of office which are key receptacles of his own power.<br /><br />1988: Dream is able to escape. Weak. Angry. Confused.<br /><br />During this first TPB of <i>The Sandman</i>, Morpheus must seek out for his tótems of power, a little pouch full of sand, a helm with the shape of a WWI gas mask, and a ruby.<br /><br />Dream needs all those three artifacts since he put inside of them too much of his own power and therefore, now without them, he won’t be able to regain control of his own realm.<br /><br />Dream will have a little help from John Constantine to get back one of those items, but about the remaining two, he must face alone dangerous challenges in unexpected places and/or against unexpected oponents.<br /><br />And since Morpheus needs those things to get back to full power, evidently he isn’t in top shape to fight his enemies with raw power, so he must rely in cunning and <i>destiny</i> to try to be again his “old self”.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8311598561522990020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Chad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25388909-chad"><img alt="Chad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589745633p2/25388909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1567814443?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chad" href="/user/show/25388909-chad">Chad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25388909-chad?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1567814443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3500815243949969622">I'm stuck at home during a pandemic so why not revisit an old friend. Sandman was the first comic I remember reading in high school where I first thought, "Hey, maybe comics aren't just for kids!". <br/><br/>Even though <i>Vertigo</i> later distanced itself from the rest of the DC universe. I love how Gaiman grounds this right in the DC universe while still making it its own thing. John Constantine who quickly become one of my favorite DC characters. Even the Justice League shows up with the main villain, Dr. De</span>
  <span id="freeText3500815243949969622" style="display:none">I'm stuck at home during a pandemic so why not revisit an old friend. Sandman was the first comic I remember reading in high school where I first thought, "Hey, maybe comics aren't just for kids!". <br /><br />Even though <i>Vertigo</i> later distanced itself from the rest of the DC universe. I love how Gaiman grounds this right in the DC universe while still making it its own thing. John Constantine who quickly become one of my favorite DC characters. Even the Justice League shows up with the main villain, Dr. Destiny, being a JLA villain. Rather than ignoring the Golden Age Sandman, Gaiman explains how the two are related. <br /><br />Gaiman does embrace a dark, horror vibe, especially in the issue where Dr. Destiny tortures the patrons of a diner. Sam Keith and Mike Dringenberg give the book a nice 1950's horror look. The book looks like it could have been issues of <i>Eerie!</i> or <i>House of Mystery</i>. Speaking of <i>House of Mystery</i> I adore how Gaiman repurposes the host of those books, Cain and Abel, making them supporting characters in Sandman. I like how he uses the idea of the Dreaming to explain those old DC horror stories.<br /><br />And then there's Death. Who would have ever thought that Death would be this adorable, perky little Goth girl, not so much menacing you as you die, but comforting and helping you on your journey to the great beyond. I'm so glad that I can go back and revisit this 30 years later and it's still just as magical as the first time I read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3500815243949969622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2293913678091429461" style="display:none">Re-Read 1/3/20:<br /><br />Just as good as the previous reads. :)<br /><br /><br />Original Review:<br /><br />I have to say I enjoyed this much better the second time around. <br /><br />Both times I really got a kick out of the Crowley sidestory, and I still have no idea who or what the original sandman comic was all about, nor do I particularly care, but this reimagining is a real work of art.<br /><br />It's not even the art, per-se, although I did enjoy seeing Bowie as Satan. It fits him so well. It was the story. It seemed to stumble about for a short while as 70 years of imprisonment turned a very old god into a shadow, but once he was released, we had the real power of myth awaken and we suddenly had a Quest to Regain Powerful Artifacts, including John Constantine representing normal human worlds, Satan representing hell, and Doctor Dee representing dreams all made perfectly real. Each success returned Dream to his former glory and established a truly epic worldbuilding experience.<br /><br />If everything is possible, then what is necessary?<br /><br />The answer? A good night's sleep. <br /><br />Possibly.<br /><br />Oh hell, I know what's going to happen later. This has made me almost impossibly giddy to revisit it. Welcome home, Dream.</span>

<div id="review_18621466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18621466" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18621466?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=graphic-novel">graphic-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=popular-culture">popular-culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=weird">weird</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18621466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18323423979170972464">Since joining <i>goodreads</i>, I’ve been baffled by the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman' title='Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neil Gaiman</a> love fest. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4407.American_Gods' title='American Gods by Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>American Gods</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere' title='Neverwhere by Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neverwhere</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust' title='Stardust by Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stardust</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book' title='The Graveyard Book by Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Graveyard Book</a>, they appear to be universally loved, and I’ve been skeptical of this emotion that borders on worship. These books are good and all, and I recognize their general accessibility, but I don’t personally find any of them mind blowing literature. Gaiman’s prose is no match for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville' title='China Miéville' rel='nofollow noopener'>China Mieville</a>’s or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7628.Iain_M__Banks' title='Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Iain M. Banks</a>’ or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/874602.Ursula_K__Le_Guin' title='Ursula K. Le Guin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ursula LeGuin</a>’s (and countless others who write speculative</span>
  <span id="freeText18323423979170972464" style="display:none">Since joining <i>goodreads</i>, I’ve been baffled by the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neil Gaiman</a> love fest. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4407.American_Gods" title="American Gods by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">American Gods</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere" title="Neverwhere by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neverwhere</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust" title="Stardust by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Stardust</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book" title="The Graveyard Book by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Graveyard Book</a>, they appear to be universally loved, and I’ve been skeptical of this emotion that borders on worship. These books are good and all, and I recognize their general accessibility, but I don’t personally find any of them mind blowing literature. Gaiman’s prose is no match for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/33918.China_Mi_ville" title="China Miéville" rel="nofollow noopener">China Mieville</a>’s or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7628.Iain_M__Banks" title="Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Iain M. Banks</a>’ or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/874602.Ursula_K__Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener">Ursula LeGuin</a>’s (and countless others who write speculative fiction), and the way he recasts mythology into contemporary settings is more clever than inspired. The love accorded Gaiman, therefore, feels disproportionate to the quality of his work – at least to me.<br /><br />Lately, however, I’ve been reminded that I once loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neil Gaiman</a>, and that reminder was my return to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23754.The_Sandman_Vol__1_Preludes_and_Nocturnes" title="The Sandman Vol. 1 Preludes and Nocturnes by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman Preludes and Nocturnes</a>. Like his other fine work, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22341.The_Wolves_in_the_Walls" title="The Wolves in the Walls by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wolves in the Walls</a>, The Sandman series plays to Gaiman’s greatest strength: his ability to conjure beautiful images from artists. But it also elevates many of the things that Gaiman is usually only able to do adequately. His writing, when confined by thought and dialogue bubbles, is inspired (mostly because its goal is to be natural and believable rather than aspiring to literary greatness); his contemporizing of mythology is much more palatable (happening, as it does, in a comic book universe predisposed to gods and heroes); and his naturally cinematic pacing works better in a graphic format. Yes, indeed...graphic novels are Neil Gaiman’s best form.<br /><br /><b>Sleep of the Just</b> – This may be the greatest first issue of a comic ever written. The capture of Morpheus/Dream/Sandman (or whichever name of his you prefer), the sleeping sickness, his inevitable (and beautifully patient) escape and vengeance guarantees that any fan of fantasy <i>or</i> comic books or fantasy <i>and</i> comic books must continue with the series. Even better, though, <i>Sleep of the Just</i> could have been its own stand-alone issue, and that would have been good enough. There are few single issues of a comic that are so fulfilling. I buy it all, and everything I <i>had</i> to know was given to me. Luckily, Gaiman left me with plenty beyond what I wanted to know. My personal favourite: the introduction of Sandman’s helm. Killer.<br /><br /><b>Imperfect Hosts</b> – A kick ass follow up episode that includes a taste of Sandman’s powers, the characters that populate his Dreamworld, and the beginning of his search for the three artefacts stolen when Burgess captured him instead of Death. This episode is most notable, however, for the way Gaiman weaves his Sandman into the existing universe of DC. I am not a DC fan. I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59960.Batman_The_Dark_Knight_Returns" title="Batman The Dark Knight Returns by Frank Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Batman</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22369.All_Star_Superman_Volume_01" title="All Star Superman Volume 01 by Grant Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Superman</a> because they are cultural requirements, and what I know of the DC Universe is filtered through the pages of those books, but Sandman was a rare piece that warped and wefted its way into the DC universe without letting itself get bogged down in DC’s usual shabbiness. <i>Imperfect Hosts</i> is where this all begins to happen.<br /><br /><b>Dream A Little Dream of Me</b> – A weakened and vulnerable Morpheus is busy looking for his sandbag, the first of the three stolen artefacts that can restore him to his former splendour and power. So he tracks down John Constantine, the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/133018.Hellblazer_Dangerous_Habits" title="Hellblazer Dangerous Habits by Garth Ennis" rel="nofollow noopener">Hellblazer</a>, who bought the sandbag years before and put it into storage, but the sandbag is gone, stolen by Constantine’s ex-lover, Rachel, a heroin addict who needed money for a fix. She never got it; instead, the sandbag took control of her mind, throwing her into a forever nightmare that included the transformation of her father into a room sized, living, breathing, tortured, mass of flesh. <i>Dream a Little Dream of Me</i> is a horror show that hints at the depths of nightmare Dream will combat in future issues, and it embeds Morpheus more deeply into the DC Universe. It’s a satisfying chapter in Morpheus’ rebirth, and this is where the patient build towards the story’s literary quality begins. <br /><br /><b>A Hope In Hell</b> – This is the one issue that really doesn’t thrill me too much. Morpheus goes to Hell and meets up with Lucifer, Beelzebub and Azazel – Hell’s triumvirate of Dark Lords – demanding the return of his helm. He ends up dueling Choronzon for his helm in a "reality" battle. Each takes a turn in the shape or form or concept of something or other. Each incarnation is slightly tougher than the opponent’s until the victor’s incarnation can’t be beat. Morpheus defeats Choronzon as "hope," which totally sucks. Hope?! Please. I can see hope as a stage in the battle, perhaps, but as the ultimate incarnation of victory? No way. Hope can be good, but it’s also an emotion that can derail thought and action -- and that makes hope potentially bad and self-defeating. Still, Lucifer was cool and his parting words about Dream give us plenty to look forward to in the series to come: “One day, my brothers...One day I shall destroy him.”<br /><br /><b>Passengers</b> – A creepy start to the search for Morpheus’ last artefact – the Ruby of Dreams. A decrepit Doctor Destiny is sufficiently mad when he escapes Arkham Asylum, Morpheus runs into J’onn and Scott Free from the JLI, and the Doctor Destiny corrupted Ruby throws Morpheus into a catatonic stupor on the floor of a storage garage in the middle of nowhere, all setting the stage for the most terrifying chapter of Volume One:<br /><br /><b>24 Hours</b> – Bloody, nasty, marvelous. Dreams in the hand of a corrupted man become corruption, and the whole Earth suffers. This is the best issue of The Sandman in Preludes and Nocturnes, so I'll let it speak for itself. But be warned: this one is not for the faint hearted.<br /><br /><b>Sound and Fury</b> – This is a satisfying resolution to Dream’s return to power. Sandman shows John Dee mercy, he bestows the Earth with a night of pleasant dreams, and he returns to his Dreamscape to rebuild his kingdom. It’s not quite as powerful as <i>24 Hours</i>, but it does what it needs to do.<br /><br /><b>The Sound of Her Wings</b> – Death is a beautiful thing. If there were no other reason to love Neil Gaiman, this realization would be enough because Death <i>really is</i> a beautiful thing -- both in the comic and at the end of our lives<br /><br />I’m glad I revisited Gaiman's greatest moment. Maybe now I can enjoy his new stuff more and appreciate him as much as so many of my friends do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18323423979170972464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_334398280" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/334398280" />
    <a title="Kirstine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine"><img alt="Kirstine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505503603p2/5172557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/334398280?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirstine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirstine" href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine">Kirstine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=1-men">1-men</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=comics-and-graphic-novels">comics-and-graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=r-2017">r-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer334398280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4516597242998087349">"<i>I am anti-life, the beast of judgement. I am the dark at the end of everything. The end of universes, Gods, worlds... of everything. And what will you be <b>then</b>, Dreamlord?</i>"<br/><br/>"<i>I am hope.</i>"<br/><br/>This is my favourite quote from this book, and one of my favourite quotes in general. It's beautiful. And true, thank god, so true. <br/><br/>I really liked this book. I've wanted to read <em>The Sandman</em> for a long time, and after this I'm beginning to grasp why it's become so popular. <br/><br/>You can tell that this is a prelude to the </span>
  <span id="freeText4516597242998087349" style="display:none">"<i>I am anti-life, the beast of judgement. I am the dark at the end of everything. The end of universes, Gods, worlds... of everything. And what will you be <b>then</b>, Dreamlord?</i>"<br /><br />"<i>I am hope.</i>"<br /><br />This is my favourite quote from this book, and one of my favourite quotes in general. It's beautiful. And true, thank god, so true. <br /><br />I really liked this book. I've wanted to read <em>The Sandman</em> for a long time, and after this I'm beginning to grasp why it's become so popular. <br /><br />You can tell that this is a prelude to the "actual" series. Morpheus, one of the Endless, master of Dreams, has been kidnapped, and once he breaks free, well, things aren't as he left them. So the first volume is all about Morpheus trying to get his power back. It's an enthralling and entertaining journey, that introduces a lot of characters, places and plots that will be revisited later. <br /><br />I enjoy Morpheus a lot as a character. He comes off as very sympathetic and yet it's soon made clear he can be as brutal and unforgiving as many others. He is Endless after all. <br /><br />It's a lot more violent than I remember it as, which is probably because I just gratefully forgot. I'm not prudish, but I have to admit the cruelty is often very difficult to watch. I suppose I'll have to get used to it, because judging by the second volume it's not slowing down.<br /><br />I'll definitely continue with this series, and now I've finally bought the whole thing. It's creative, the art is to die for, and it manages to be both dark and hopeful at the same time. Well, most of the time...</span>
  <a data-text-id="4516597242998087349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_934355377" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/934355377" />
    <a title="Will M." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20408288-will-m"><img alt="Will M." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452951660p2/20408288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/934355377?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will M." href="/user/show/20408288-will-m">Will M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer934355377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11201208771721490077">One of those graphic novels that are so famous and hyped up that it makes you want to read them. The consequence though would be that expectations tend to rocket up. I am more disappointed than entertained.<br/><br/>I've read 2 novels of Neil Gaiman. I hated Stardust, while Ocean was a mediocre read for me. I DNF(did-not-finish) American Gods and Graveyard Book because I got bored. The Sandman was not that great for me. I think I have to conclude soon enough that I honestly don't see what's so good about </span>
  <span id="freeText11201208771721490077" style="display:none">One of those graphic novels that are so famous and hyped up that it makes you want to read them. The consequence though would be that expectations tend to rocket up. I am more disappointed than entertained.<br /><br />I've read 2 novels of Neil Gaiman. I hated Stardust, while Ocean was a mediocre read for me. I DNF(did-not-finish) American Gods and Graveyard Book because I got bored. The Sandman was not that great for me. I think I have to conclude soon enough that I honestly don't see what's so good about Gaiman. I just don't get his weird writing. I didn't hate this first volume, but it was not mind blowing. <br /><br />The terrible thing about this volume would be the overwhelming amount of things happening. There were too many characters (both new and recycled) so some ended up flat and boring. The main character was not bad, but like I said, he became a bit flat and boring. The only one that I really liked would be John Constantine. I hated Martian Manhunter ever since I was a child, and I still hated him after reading this.<br /><br />The plot was also a bit overwhelming and confusing at times. So many things were happening, and some ideas that you want the author to expound on got overlooked because new ideas were presented. The overall story was actually interesting, so I'll see if it improves in the second volume.<br /><br />3/5 stars. Still disappointed in Gaiman, but hopefully the second volume would be an improvement over this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11201208771721490077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57663943" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57663943" />
    <a title="Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1720620-anne"><img alt="Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415374035p2/1720620.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57663943?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne" href="/user/show/1720620-anne">Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57663943" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8265783313495174816">I liked the beginning, when Dream was captured. The mystical elements held my attention. However, the middle of it kind of grossed me out. Especially the part in with Dee in the diner. EWWWW. The end of Preludes and Nocturnes totally made up for it, though. I loved Death! How cool is she?! Whoever came up with the idea to make Death a peppy little goth girl was a genius! </span>

<div id="review_2519842660" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2519842660" />
    <a title="Johann (jobis89)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50893023-johann-jobis89"><img alt="Johann (jobis89)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588540681p2/50893023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2519842660?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johann (jobis89)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johann (jobis89)" href="/user/show/50893023-johann-jobis89">Johann (jobis89)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2519842660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7591962139457804500">“I am hope.”<br/><br/>A man named Roderick Burgress wants to prevent death in the world by summoning and capturing Death – but what he ends up with is Dream instead. Dream is imprisoned for 70 years and loses his tools that give him his power. Upon Dream’s release he must embark on a journey to retrieve his tools before the world falls into turmoil.<br/><br/>I’ve been so psyched to read the Sandman series since I first heard about it on bookstagram. It’s considered by many to be the pinnacle of graphic novels - an </span>
  <span id="freeText7591962139457804500" style="display:none">“I am hope.”<br /><br />A man named Roderick Burgress wants to prevent death in the world by summoning and capturing Death – but what he ends up with is Dream instead. Dream is imprisoned for 70 years and loses his tools that give him his power. Upon Dream’s release he must embark on a journey to retrieve his tools before the world falls into turmoil.<br /><br />I’ve been so psyched to read the Sandman series since I first heard about it on bookstagram. It’s considered by many to be the pinnacle of graphic novels - an epic, sprawling masterpiece that might even be Gaiman’s best work. My first impression was that the artwork is dark and gritty, just how I like it—and hey, doesn’t Dream look a lot like Gaiman himself? But I was hooked pretty quickly.<br /><br />Gaiman’s imagination will never fail to impress me, he is insanely creative and his ability to incorporate mythology into contemporary settings is extremely clever. This volume does really feel like a “prelude” to the entire story, a prolonged introduction almost. We get to meet Dream aka Morpheus aka The Sandman (choose whichever name you prefer) and as a character, I find him incredibly intriguing. He is a sympathetic character, but can be brutal when he feels it’s necessary. We also meet a few other characters who you get the impression are going to become huge players within the series – his sister Death and then Lucifer himself. Excited to learn more about both of these characters!<br /><br />My favourite issue in this volume was 24 Hours. It’s gory and graphic and you won’t want to stop reading until you’re done. <br /><br />What annoyed me slightly was the inclusion of some DC characters, I haven’t read any of these comics so their significance was pretty much lost on me. I’m not sure if having prior knowledge really matters or not, but I hate the feeling that I’m missing out on something.<br /><br />I’m really looking forward to continuing with this series as I feel like the real story is only beginning…</span>
  <a data-text-id="7591962139457804500" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2958722582" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2958722582" />
    <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse"><img alt="Ms. Smartarse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401273675p2/13703358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2958722582?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. Smartarse" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse">Ms. Smartarse</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ms. Smartarse by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/21223898-roxana-chiril">Roxana Chirilă</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=graphic-novel">graphic-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=rave">rave</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=cover-love">cover-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=late_xx_century">late_xx_century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=psychological">psychological</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=part_of_a_series">part_of_a_series</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2958722582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17701238985675168690">Dream (a.k.a. <em>the Sandman</em>) is mistakenly captured by a creepy cult, that had been hoping to bind <em>Death</em>. Attempting to make the most of their failure, the cultists try to persuade Dream to do their bidding, but he refuses to even acknowledge them. Instead, he bides his time until his captors make a mistake... 72 years later.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1574949889i/28523930.jpg' alt='failed capture' width='350' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I loved the story, especially the first chapter where the effects of Dream's imprisonment on humanity are depicted. For all that I'm not big on horror and gore, I was positive</span>
  <span id="freeText17701238985675168690" style="display:none">Dream (a.k.a. <em>the Sandman</em>) is mistakenly captured by a creepy cult, that had been hoping to bind <em>Death</em>. Attempting to make the most of their failure, the cultists try to persuade Dream to do their bidding, but he refuses to even acknowledge them. Instead, he bides his time until his captors make a mistake... 72 years later.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1574949889i/28523930.jpg" alt="failed capture" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I loved the story, especially the first chapter where the effects of Dream's imprisonment on humanity are depicted. For all that I'm not big on horror and gore, I was positively fascinated with all the nitty-gritty details that both the story and the illustrations provided. I was so enthralled with it all, that I was simultaneously wishing for chapters not to end, as well as looking forward to the next step in Dream's quest.<br><br>There was really only one downside, when it came to fully enjoying the reading experience. Two of the more important chapters featured cameos from some minor(?) DC characters, most of whom I had no knowledge of. Before you jump on me for calling the likes of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/characters/24482-john-constantine" rel="nofollow noopener">John Constantine</a> a minor character, please keep in mind that most of my DC universe knowledge comes from a few episodes of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batman:_The_Animated_Series" rel="nofollow noopener">the animated Batman series</a>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1574961803i/28525108.jpg" alt="John Constantine" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Initially, I had only meant to briefly flip through "<em>Preludes and Nocturnes</em>", vaguely recalling that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/21223898" rel="nofollow noopener">Roxana</a> had recommended the series. In the end, I found myself eagerly carting around the first 5 volumes, while waiting to board a 14-hour flight. And let me tell you, they're much heavier than they look.<br><br>The longer I thought about the upcoming reading experience, the more my excitement started to vane. What if I had paid a small fortune just for some pretty covers? The last time I tried my hand at an <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9615347.Anya_s_Ghost" title="Anya's Ghost by Vera Brosgol" rel="nofollow noopener">over-hyped graphic novel</a>, I finished reading it in an hour, and gave it away 3 days later... <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1574949889i/28523931.jpg" alt="first 5 volumes" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It eventually took the launching of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46227117.Sandman__1__Preludii__i_nocturne" title="Sandman #1. Preludii și nocturne by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Romanian translation</a>, and a <a href="https://www.cnet.com/news/the-sandman-tv-series-for-netflix-will-have-a-more-modern-settting-says-neil-gaiman/" rel="nofollow noopener">notice of an upcoming Netflix series</a> to finally push me into giving it a try. Even then, I proceeded most carefully, prolonging the experience for as long as I could. What can I say? Bragging rights spur me on like nothing else.<br><br><strong>Score: 4.3/5 stars</strong><br>When it comes to favorite quotes, I wouldn't have thought it possible that graphic novels could ever supply one, yet this one here has to be my absolute favorite from the entire book.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1574951091i/28524068.jpg" alt="storytellers and their monsters huddled under the bed" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><em>Awesome both in AND out of context.</em><br><br>I actually ended up reading the first volume twice, hoping to write a more inspiring review. While I <em>did</em> enjoy the story more the 2nd time around, I'm not sure I can get more eloquent than: "<em>WOW!</em>" and possibly "<em>I wish I was more familiar with the DC universe</em>".<br><br>====================<br>Review of volume 2: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3013180156" rel="nofollow noopener">The Doll's House</a><br>Review of volume 3: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3013179860" rel="nofollow noopener">Dream Country</a><br>Review of volume 4: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3585131781" rel="nofollow noopener">Season of Mists</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17701238985675168690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText9176666242894483417" style="display:none">Preludes and Nocturnes is the first entry of Neil Gaiman’s The Sandman series and in it he introduces the main protagonist, Dream, also known as Morpheus, and also unveils an imaginative storyline. <br><br>Written in collaboration with graphic novel artists Sam Kieth, Mike Dringenberg, and Malcolm Jones III, the medium illuminates his creativity and expands the reader’s perceptions of his vision. <br><br>The Sandman concept promises an occult, fantastic journey into dreams, Hell, and an intimate association with Death, with anthropomorphic personifications reminiscent of Piers Anthony.<br><br>Lots of fun.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1560201159i/27648148._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
