<span id="freeTextContainer3271474269956504608">Why, hello. Sandman, Morpheus, Lord of Dreams. This is the first we see of Gaiman's Eternal (one of seven god-like beings).<br/><br/>In this first volume we see how <s>Alastair Crowley</s> High Mage Burgess accidentally captures Dream instead of his sister Death and imprisons him for the next 70 years or so. However, you can't just remove an Eternal as they have responsibilities. Thus, the world and everyone in it suffers severely. Since Dream refuses to give Burgess what he craves, he remains a prisoner until l</span>
  <span id="freeText3271474269956504608" style="display:none">Why, hello. Sandman, Morpheus, Lord of Dreams. This is the first we see of Gaiman's Eternal (one of seven god-like beings).<br><br>In this first volume we see how <s>Alastair Crowley</s> High Mage Burgess accidentally captures Dream instead of his sister Death and imprisons him for the next 70 years or so. However, you can't just remove an Eternal as they have responsibilities. Thus, the world and everyone in it suffers severely. Since Dream refuses to give Burgess what he craves, he remains a prisoner until long after Burgess is dead. Then, an opportunity presents itself and he breaks free.<br>The problem? When they locked him up, they took three objects from him that hold part of his power. Fans of a certain young wizard will recognize the idea of storing part of your soul/power in objects - and no, I don't mind if the author actually took her idea from this one and made it her own. Because this is different. Because Dream is ... well, eternal as the name suggests (so he doesn't store parts of himself in objects for the same reason as YouKnowWho). He doesn't die until the end of all things when his sister turns off the lights and closes up shop behind everyone. Instead, it's about his abilities. And, naturally, he needs to get the objects back in order to repair the damage done to the world during his absence.<br>Thus we follow him on a quest to retrieve a pouch filled with dream sand, a ruby and his mask.<br><br>Since this is volume 1 of 10, I think we all can guess how the quest goes. However, as is most often the case, the way is much more important than the final destination (excuse the pun).<br>And along the way, here, we meet other DC characters such as John Constantine or Lucifer Morningstar before even someone as powerful as an Eternal gets dressed down by his older sister. *sniggers*<br><br>Seriously, I love Dream but I LOVE Death. She is so cool (and not just because of her love for <i>Mary Poppins</i>).<br><br>*clears throat*<br>Back to the main story though: The art is nothing spectacular. Sometimes it's borderline ugly even. I'm told it is the typical art of 1989. I wouldn't know. But the story. Oh, the story! It's solid as hell (sorry, another pun). It's riveting, thrilling, funny in a very macabre sort of way, and deeply mystical. There are A LOT of references to history, mythology, movies / TV shows and other DC comics. I had to look up one or two such references, but mostly I understood because Neil Gaiman probably wanted to ensure anyone could follow and enjoy this tale.<br><br>So don't hold your breath regarding the panels, but don't let that discourage you; do get sucked into the Realm of Dreams by the quality of the writing. I definitely will and can't wait for the next adventure.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578086410i/28707278.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="3271474269956504608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1710121312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1493775702004673535">I first read this a couple of years ago, and it was one of the first graphic novels I read. Even though I love me some Gaiman, I have to admit that I didn’t love this. It seemed so incredibly dark when I first read it. Since that time, I’ve read many more graphic novels, most of which have been dark. I decided to revisit this series to see if I still found as dark as I did originally. <br/><br/>Yes. Yes, I do.<br/><br/>The plot of the first few issues didn’t bother me. Dream’s story and search were compelling. Wher</span>
  <span id="freeText1493775702004673535" style="display:none">I first read this a couple of years ago, and it was one of the first graphic novels I read. Even though I love me some Gaiman, I have to admit that I didn’t love this. It seemed so incredibly dark when I first read it. Since that time, I’ve read many more graphic novels, most of which have been dark. I decided to revisit this series to see if I still found as dark as I did originally. <br /><br />Yes. Yes, I do.<br /><br />The plot of the first few issues didn’t bother me. Dream’s story and search were compelling. Where I got uncomfortable was when Doctor Destiny became the central focus. That issue remains one of the darkest things I’ve ever read. <br /><br />You can tell when reading Sandman that it’s written by a novelist. The writing is much more dense than that to which I’ve become accustomed. You can also tell that it was written decades ago. The writing and the art are dated. Artistically, my favorite aspect of Sandman is the almost abstract styling of the issue covers.<br /><br />I can see why this is a cult classic, but I’m afraid that it’s a cult that I won’t be joining, despite my second attempt at trying to develop a taste for it. If you’re a Sandman lover, more power to you, and please accept my sincerest apologies for not sharing that love.</span>

  <span id="freeText11471299396309043971" style="display:none">In the foreword and the afterword, both the editor and Gaiman indicated that this isn't the strongest volume in the Sandman series; Gaiman was still finding his vision for the series, it's essentially a fetch quest, etc. I'm inclined to agree with them, although it was still enjoyable (as any video game fan will tell you, a fetch quest can still be fun, but it's not the strongest narrative device). I'm halfway through Volume 2: The Doll's House and I'm already finding the story much more interesting. Preludes and Nocturnes is a good introduction, but not much more than that.</span>

<div id="review_1039496898" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1039496898" />
    <a title="Hershey " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19468761-hershey"><img alt="Hershey " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599546236p2/19468761.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1039496898?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hershey " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hershey " href="/user/show/19468761-hershey">Hershey </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19468761-hershey?shelf=heights-of-awesomeness">heights-of-awesomeness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19468761-hershey?shelf=fun-fun-fun">fun-fun-fun</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19468761-hershey?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19468761-hershey?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19468761-hershey?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19468761-hershey?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1039496898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11088310318712930394">Once I was all done with this book, my very first thought was "This book is really weird."<br/><br/>Neil Gaiman, as all of you know, is a God at creating the most intricate and best of all story worlds. Honestly, every time I pick up his books, I never have a single clue with where he's going to take the readers with his story lines. Reading the blurb seriously does not help. Gaiman's works always have depth and much more stuff going on than what the blurb normally says.<br/><br/>So, I finally have gotten around to</span>
  <span id="freeText11088310318712930394" style="display:none">Once I was all done with this book, my very first thought was "This book is really weird."<br><br>Neil Gaiman, as all of you know, is a God at creating the most intricate and best of all story worlds. Honestly, every time I pick up his books, I never have a single clue with where he's going to take the readers with his story lines. Reading the blurb seriously does not help. Gaiman's works always have depth and much more stuff going on than what the blurb normally says.<br><br>So, I finally have gotten around to picking up one of his most hyped books or it sounds better when put this way, one of the most hyped graphic novels of all time, The Sandman. Of course, when I opened the book I immediately wanted to love this book like I always think whenever I start reading an extremely hyped book. Plus, it's by Neil Gaiman and I loved the two books of his I read recently so yes, I was very much prepared to love this book.<br><br>Everything went on really great considering half the time I was trying the figure out what the hell was going on. With the dream sequences intervening the story line, it was kind of hard to follow but ehh I managed quite well, I guess. Except for the last bit. If not for that, this would have gotten a solid 5 star rating. But we'll come to that a bit later.<br><br>I feel like that going into this graphic novel without knowing much would provide the best reading experience but I will give you the gist of the story line. Feel free to skip the next paragraph if you're interested in going into the graphic novel without knowing much about it. <br><br>So, there are these people who decide to summon Death and cure the disease of dying. But alas, their summoning does not bring Death but another kind of Endless. Their summoning brought the King of Dreams, The Sandman. They ask him for wishes but he never talks to them. Many, many years pass and finally the Sandman makes his escape from the glass prison his human captors have created for him. He had three tools into which he put most of his magic and now they're missing, stolen by his human captors and passed onto others. The first volume, Preludes and Nocturnes is about Sandman recovering his most prized tools. <br><br>At times, there was a lot happening in the graphic novel and it was really hard to follow because after Sandman's capture and his tools going missing, people don't have proper dreams. They have weird dreams, like how dreams are always like and these dream sequences keep interrupting the story line and many at times, it threw me off balance with what was actually happening. It was a slight inconvenience but it did not annoy me or made me want to rip this book. I just wish I understood it better but people say it gets easier to understand as you keep on reading the volumes. I guess you just get used to it. After all, this is a story about the King of Dreams and dreams generally don't make any sense.<br><br>Also, I'm really addicted to how the King of Dreams looks like in Gaiman's graphic novels. The Sandman looks a bit like him, don't you think? <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425210369i/13862136._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I really, really, dig this look. I love this version of Sandman! He's so sexy! :D<br><br>Coming back to the story line, you also get to see certain DC characters and it was really cool how Gaiman incorporated those characters into this graphic novel. There was a lot going on in this first volume and the last bit was hella confusing but hey I know dreams are fucking incomprehensible and I decided to go with it. Maybe,after reading the sequels and years later when I pick this amazing book again, I'll be like, "Yep. I get it!".<br><br>Let's not forget the artwork! The artwork for this book was very dark and gritty. The colors were stark and never soft and at times, a lot of stuff happens in one page which might make it quite hard to follow. Sometimes, you wanna stare at the plethora of characters in a single page and other times you just want to get on with it. The illustrations weren't swoon-worthy and they weren't bad either. It was good. I guess more importance was given to the Sandman and his story lines which I was completely fine with.<br><br>I know my review makes me sound like I'm torn between liking and disliking this book, but to be honest, I really did enjoy this book a lot. It had so many characters (some very familiar), so many ideas, so much depth and on the whole, I had a lot of fun reading it even when it was confusing as hell. It didn't even bother me much. Just don't ask to me explain every single thing, okay? :D<br><br>Sample:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425210721i/13862191.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="11088310318712930394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1049775089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9281918152364441803">Decided to re-read this since my library had it. I haven't read this since the 90s. I don't remember anything about it so far. It's kind of like all brand new and sparkly. And creepy. Yes, definitely creepy, in a good way.<br/><br/>And now here's a random Bowie gif that reminded me of this for some reason:<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1427068919ra/14172380.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I'm keeping my original rating. This was fantastic. I like how he worked a few superheroes into it and <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[his goth sister ended up being Death. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> This is adult comics at its bes</span>
  <span id="freeText9281918152364441803" style="display:none">Decided to re-read this since my library had it. I haven't read this since the 90s. I don't remember anything about it so far. It's kind of like all brand new and sparkly. And creepy. Yes, definitely creepy, in a good way.<br><br>And now here's a random Bowie gif that reminded me of this for some reason:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1427068919ra/14172380.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I'm keeping my original rating. This was fantastic. I like how he worked a few superheroes into it and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[his goth sister ended up being Death. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> This is adult comics at its best, for sure. So far, I much prefer Gaiman's Sandman to any of his full-length books. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427164211i/14242178._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

<div id="review_434836425" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/434836425" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/434836425?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401225759" href="/book/show/6657541-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=gn-superhero-scifi-fantasy">gn-superhero-scifi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=gn-course-f-14">gn-course-f-14</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434836425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13283867147380855710">7/5/2020 Third reading of this volume, whiich used to be the opening volume of the classic series until Overture was published decades later as a kind of prequel to the series. Yet this volume and some of the next contain prefatory material, or what Gaiman calls "a little night music." As with many series, it is a part of the process of finding its way, getting the elements or the chemistry right. And I think both the storytelling and the artistic choices were less than ideal for this beginning </span>
  <span id="freeText13283867147380855710" style="display:none">7/5/2020 Third reading of this volume, whiich used to be the opening volume of the classic series until Overture was published decades later as a kind of prequel to the series. Yet this volume and some of the next contain prefatory material, or what Gaiman calls "a little night music." As with many series, it is a part of the process of finding its way, getting the elements or the chemistry right. And I think both the storytelling and the artistic choices were less than ideal for this beginning of one of the great comics series of all time. It is interesting that below I say I had been hard hit at the reading of this by the deaths of two people who had been close to me, and today, my sister died, and I finished reading the very last section, the most moving part, the one where Death takes several people away, just now, in tears. <br /><br />In case you didn't know, Sandman was a minor character in the DC universe. The series editor that gave him the go-ahead on the projec, Karen Berger, told him, okay, sure, Sandman, but make him a completely new character. Boy, did he!<br /><br />Mr. Sandman, sung by The Chordettes:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CX45pYvxDiA">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CX45p...</a><br /><br />10/2/14, my initial review, somewhat edited: Second reading of this book, which I initially had a hard time with, and was confused, and found ambitious and impressive in its erudite attempt to incorporate myth and fantasy and horror in its celebration of dream/imagination/story but not.. intimate and warm, taken up with disturbing horror as it does... and all of that is true for me this time, though as others have observed, the introduction of Death in the last section, "The Sound of her Wings," is so refreshing and disarming and comforting, such a light-at-the-end of-the-tunnel relief after all the intense quest-like action, that I realize that here is the warmth and death and joy that underlies Gaiman's work, the thing that is almost innocent and naive in its embrace of story against the onslaught of the horrors of the world, the psychotic sociopathic evil that we are faced with daily in one way or the other. I suddenly realize how it is he able to write these children's books like Chu's Day and The Wolves at the Door and I Swapped by Dad for Two Goldfish, because at the heart of this work, Sandman, is this innocence. Sandman is not Chu's Day, but they are about his hopefulness at their core, maybe. This week I have been hit, heavy-hearted, with two deaths of people close to me, and the reading of the last, death section, was almost a kind of relief.. to hear this kicky goth girl talk about how much she loves Mary Poppins, well, you gotta be there, you'll have yourself a smile. No brooding cloaked dude with a scythe, this girl Death, let me tell ya. In the very last pages as Death "takes" a few people, as she surely will, there's a few touching moments that make you realize this will be an amazing series.<br /><br />So this is sometimes a rocky and sometimes an impressive opening, a kind of simple quest story of Dream--who had been imprisoned for 70 years by an occult leader hoping to imprison Death and attain eternal life--regaining and asserting his power. That Dream looks like Gaiman did, as a couple reviewers have pointed out, is thoughtful and insightful in its connecting Morpheus the Dreamlord with Gaiman the storyteller who dreams up whole worlds for our pleasure and horror. But pleasure, in any case. <br /><br />Gaiman himself he found rereading his own early work, decades later, to be a little awkward and clumsy, and the series editor Karen Berger in her preface and many reviewers agree, he hadn't really found his voice until he wrote the sister-brother meeting of Death and Dream, but what a voice he finally forges for us when he does.<br /><br />One problem with some of the early issues is the pulpy, goofy artwork of Sam Kieth; it seems very clear to me that fewer cooks would have been better in the making of this soup. Dave McKean, who does the covers throughout, creates the kind of gravitas and surrealism that fits the tone Gaiman wants, whereas Sam Kieth leans to a style owing something to Mort Drucker (Mad Magazine) or Will Eisner, too cartoonish. But I do like it that in each issue he attempts different kinds of fantasy/horror storytelling genres: English horror story, dark fantasy. But hang in there, this gets way better!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13283867147380855710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1501139251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1501139251" />
    <a title="Anuradha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48159983-anuradha"><img alt="Anuradha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590872045p2/48159983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1501139251?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anuradha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anuradha" href="/user/show/48159983-anuradha">Anuradha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/22171942-the-sandman-vol-01">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=all-the-stars-in-the-universe">all-the-stars-in-the-universe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=comics-graphic">comics-graphic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=dissssstopia">dissssstopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=drama-drama-drama">drama-drama-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=it-s-a-kind-of-magic">it-s-a-kind-of-magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=may-the-force-be-with-you">may-the-force-be-with-you</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=thrillllllls-and-chillllllls">thrillllllls-and-chillllllls</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=literary-gasm">literary-gasm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=masters-of-mystery">masters-of-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48159983-anuradha?shelf=men-i-fell-in-love-with">men-i-fell-in-love-with</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1501139251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13084749571593470120"><b><u>POPSugar Reading Challenge:</u> #39. The first book in a series I haven't read before</b><br/><br/><b>
  <i>The air is musty, tired, old, it smells of lost dreams and rotten fabric.</i>
</b> <br/><br/>I would have to sell my kidney and part of my liver to acquire this. Fortunately, I have a friend who has a copy, so my organs are safe. Now, whether or not he sold his organs to buy this is unknown to me, but I am glad he has the book. <br/><br/>Now, I am no longer a Gaiman virgin; I've read a fair few of his riveting stories. And yet, his knowledg</span>
  <span id="freeText13084749571593470120" style="display:none"><b><u>POPSugar Reading Challenge:</u> #39. The first book in a series I haven't read before</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>The air is musty, tired, old, it smells of lost dreams and rotten fabric.</i>
</b> <br /><br />I would have to sell my kidney and part of my liver to acquire this. Fortunately, I have a friend who has a copy, so my organs are safe. Now, whether or not he sold his organs to buy this is unknown to me, but I am glad he has the book. <br /><br />Now, I am no longer a Gaiman virgin; I've read a fair few of his riveting stories. And yet, his knowledge, and also the way he uses his knowledge of mythology and combines it with humour and fantasy never ceases to baffle me. Also, because I haven't per se reviewed a comic or a graphic novel before, I must take this opportunity to admit that it's a <b>
  <i>lot</i>
</b> harder than reviewing a novel or any other book with just words, mostly because there's so much more going on! I've read comic books for a long time now, and though I see myself as more of a Marvel girl, I found myself rubbing my hands in glee each time I saw a familiar name. <br /><br />I love pop culture references, subtle double entendres, and this book, much like every book by Gaiman is chock-full of them. Mortals, belonging to the Order of Ancient Mysteries, more specifically, Roderick Burgess, Lord Magus of the Order, accidentally captures Dream, in his attempt to capture his older sister Death and become all-powerful. <b>
  <i>Wych Cross, England. Roderick Burgess's waking dreams are full of the power and the glory. And of Death, of course. Especially Death.</i>
</b> <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"They will no longer make jokes Alex. When Death is at my command."</i>
</b><br /><br />Of course, as is the result of such fundamentally system-altering act, things begin to get really weird. Of course, because it's Neil Gaiman writing for DC, it becomes weirder than weird. <b>
  <i>People fell asleep and did not wake up... They lived their lives like sleepwalkers; eating if fed, sometimes talking nonsense, dream stuff...</i>
</b> People like Unity Kinkaid <b>
  <i>for whom the Sands of Time stopped flowing sometime half a century earlier</i>
</b> lived like this for seventy long years. Not dead, but not really alive either. The Universe did try to compensate, <b>
  <i>the Universe knows someone is missing and slowly it attempts to replace him</i>
</b>, what with Wesley Dodds, who <b>
  <i>sleeps the Sleep of the Just.</i>
</b> With the death of <b>
  <i>Roderick Burgess 1863-1947 Not Dead, only Sleeping</i>
</b>, his son Alex comes to power as the...master (?) of Dream, until, as luck would have it, Dream escapes. What are mortals to the Endless, after all?<br /><br /><b>
  <i>"Confined in a glass box for three score years and ten. A human lifetime. Time moves no faster for my kind than it does for humanity, and in prison it crawled at a snail's pace... I was...I am...the Lord of this realm of Dream and Nightmare."</i>
</b><br /><br />After punishing Alex as he very well should, <b>
  <i>"It was more tiring than I had expected. But he will never return to the life he knew. His is the nightmare everlasting...Eternal waking"</i>
</b>, and chastising humans for being utter eejits, <b>
  <i>"What? You wanted Death? Then consider yourself lucky for the sake of your species and your petty planet that you did not succeed...that instead you snared Death's younger brother..."</i>
</b> our hero tries to get his life (and by extension, that of humanity) back in order. <br /><br />Dream breaks the fourth wall, so to speak, and his inner monologue is dignified, noble even, at the same time, very dry and humourless. <b>
  <i>"I have always been solitary, but here on the nightward shores of dream, loneliness washes over me in waves, lapping and pulling at my spirit."</i>
</b> His thoughts are mostly deep and profound, <b>
  <i>"Beyond, outside my dreamworld there is infinite dust, infinite dark. And the dreamworld is infinite, although it is bounded on every side"</i>
</b> and if I'd known him personally, I'd probably not have been too fond of him. He was, in simple words, boring. <b>
  <i>"The dreams that pass through the Gates of Ivory are lies, figments, and deceptions. The other admits the Truth. No one guards the horned gate anymore. I remember the Way of the Old."</i>
</b> <br />Wise? Yes. <b>
  <i>You threatened, cajoled and pleaded for gifts that are neither mankind's to receive, nor mine to give. You had no thought to the harm you must have brought to your world... Lord, what fools these mortals be."</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>"I find myself wondering about humanity. Their attitude to my sister's gift is so strange. Why do they fear the sunless lands? It is as natural to die as it is to be born. But they fear her, dread her. Feebly they attempt to placate her. They do not love her. ... My sister has a function to perform, even as I do. The Endless have their responsibilities. I have responsibilities.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>"It is never "only a dream", John Constantine. Here less than some other places...</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>All is real. Nothing is real.</i>
</b><br /><br />And of course, my personal favourite. <b>
  <i>First food. Then clothing</i>
</b> Because always, always, food wins the battle. As it should.<br /><br />Kindly? Yes. <b>
  <i>"Through your dreams, my sleeping children, you had a passenger, and you never knew."</i>
</b><br /><br />But boring? Also, yes. <b>
  <i>I left a monarch, yet I return naked, alone, hungry.</i>
</b> <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"I sift it (sand) through my fingers. Feel each grain of it. Inexhaustible. Endless. Like myself, like the few others of my kind. Endless."</i>
</b><br /><br />Nevertheless, I found myself chuckling as he chalked out his, er, plan, to get his belongings back. Because, of course, who else was involved but the Justice League of America. <b>
  <i>"There are things I do not know about this 'Justice League'. More than mere humans, eh...? The Englishman, then, John Constantine. He has the pouch--or he knows where it is. And he is just a man. ... He is, after all, just a human. Just one human. What could possibly go wrong?"</i>
</b><br /><br />Neil Gaiman seamlessly transitions between the inner monologues of his many...narrators, and the end result is beautiful. Take Constantine, for instance. <b>
  <i>"These things with faces like appendectomy scars were crocheting my intestines into body bags for the blind and dead. I told myself it was only a dream, but it did not matter. The bastards just kept on bloody knitting."</i>
</b> He says of a nightmare. For something that is supposed to give <b>
  <i>me</i>
</b> nightmares, I found myself grinning instead. I nearly peed myself when he makes a Swamp Thing reference. <b>
  <i>"I ought to introduce you to the big green bloke.You'd like him. He hasn't got a sense of humour either."</i>
</b> Of course, because of my familiarity with John Constantine, and also because he's tons more fun than Dream, I had more fun where Constantine was the narrator. His inner monologue was human, sometimes redundant, yet completely relevant. <b>
  <i>"One thing I've learned - you can know anything. It's all there. You just have to find it.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>'S weird: Smells are a hotline to memory.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>"Uh, we'll stick together, won't we?"</i>
</b>, he says at one point, because he thinks he's in a real life horror movie, and I find myself thinking "ya know, that's <i>exactly</i> what I would say."<br /><br />He sasses Dream more than once, and because of Dream's stoic demeanour, it's hilarious. <b>
  <i>"Anyway, I was fond of Rachel once. She was, you know, the girl of my dreams. For a while.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>"So. Where are you going now?" "To Hell..." "Hehhh. Aren't we all, mate? Aren't we all?</i>
</b><br /><br />And of course, I should probably end with a bit of Constantine wisdom, because we all need it. <b>
  <i>You don't call him (Dream). His kind just turn up out of the blue. They call you.</i>
</b><br /><br />Dream, in his Dreamland is assisted by many, including the proverbial (should I say Biblical?) Cain and Abel. <b>
  <i>"I'm Abel my Lord. From the first, hmm, story. The, er, victim."</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>"That's me, yer Worship. Purveyor of Penny Dreadfuls, Shilling shockers, blood and thunders and Fust-rate nightmares."</i>
</b><br /><br />In the nature of Shakespearean jesters, Cain and Abel provide some much needed comic relief. <b>
  <i>You...Button Burster! You low-down, spying, peeking, prying, butterfingered-"</i>
</b>, until of course, everything turns dark again. <b>
  <i>Even the pain feels better than the cold of death.</i>
</b><br /><br />Hell is Hell, as it should be, <b>
  <i>We travel to the summit past vasty halls that echo of screams and grunts and sighs and dust. Up the stairs that run with sweet blood</i>
</b>, but with his classic wisdom, Dream bests everyone. <b>
  <i>"What power would Hell have if those here imprisoned were not able to dream of heaven?"</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>There are many ways to lose the oldest game. Failure of nerve, hesitation... Being unable to shift into a defensive shape. Lack of imagination.</i>
</b><br /><br />My favourite part, of course is with Dr. Destiny. <b>
  <i>"Dee is for lots of things. Death. Dust. Darkness. Demons."</i>
</b> Confined to Arkham Asylum (there's a cameo appearance by the Scarecrow, <b>
  <i>""It is a comfort in wretchedness to have companions in woe." Marlowe. Faust. Of course, he was talking about Hell. But it applies equally to Arkham. Heheh"</i>
</b> by the way, and it is splendid) after his dirty deeds, Johnny Dee escapes, with a little help from his mother. In all his incredible insane glory, Dr. Destiny is a delight to read about. <br /><b>
  <i>"What are you waiting for?" "Oh, you know. The usual. The end of the world."</i>
</b> There is <i>something</i> about these classic villains that make them so...lovable. <br /><br />Of course, the materioptikon makes an appearance, though that's not his weapon of choice here. It's more powerful, more destructive than that<b>
  <i>"So go my little love. Touch the world. Eat their hearts and poison their dreams. Rip their nightmares into the daylight and scum their sleep with creeping fear."</i>
</b> A part of me wanted to see him win, just for kicks, but well, that would've been rather sad after a point. <b>
  <i>Listen: you can hear the screaming. ... Listen. Listen: You can hear sobbing. ... Listen to the anguish of a world in which the bad things are coming out of the dark places. Listen to a world in pain. Listen. You can hear it. ... Listen to the rushing rivers of blood, flowing downwards in a warm torrent. The blood of the weak. Of the helpless. Of the mad. Listen. You can hear it. ... Silence washes like a river over Arkham. No sounds of screaming, no sobbing, no noises of pain or madness. Just peace. The only noise is the gentle, even cadence of people asleep. In, out, in, out. Listen. You can hear it."</i>
</b><br /><br />Dee is, of course, full of his own kind of wisdom. <b>
  <i>"People think dreams aren't real because they aren't made of matter, of particles. Dreams are real. But they are made of viewpoints of images, of memories and puns and lost hopes."</i>
</b> And of course, full of his dreams to become the king of the world. <b>
  <i>"I'm the King of Dreams. Of everything. But it's funny. I always thought when I became King...I thought there would be applause. I thought someone would say something."</i>
</b> <br /><br />Death, Dream's older sister, finally makes an appearance. I love her! From the way she reprimands her brother for being a self-pitying arse <b>
  <i>"You are utterly the stupidest, most self-centered, appallingest excuse for an anthropomorphic personification on this or any other plane. An infantile, adolescent, pathetic specimen. Feeling all sorry for yourself because your little game is over, and you haven't got the--the balls to go and find a new one!"</i>
</b> to the way she handles the delicate nature of her job, <b>
  <i>"...Gets me down too. Mostly they aren't too keen to see me. They fear the sunless lands. But they enter your realm each night without fear." "And I am far more terrible than you, sister"</i>
</b>, Death was pretty damn cool. <br /><br />Here's some food for thought for aspiring writers: <b>
  <i>All Bette's stories have happy endings. That's because she knows where to stop. She's realized the real problem with stories. If you keep them going long enough, they always end in death.</i>
</b> Or not. I just liked the quote, honestly. <br /><br />And finally, the best part. Pop culture references. <br /><b>
  <i>"Said Tweedledum, "When you're only one of the things in his dream, you know very well you're not real.""</i>
</b> Every kid needs <i>Alice in Wonderland</i>. Just like every story needs a raven reference. <b>
  <i>"The Raven Woman has decayed badly. She lives only in nightmares...</i>
</b><br /><br />We see Paul McGuire reading Stephen King's <i>IT</i> at some point. <br /><br />Ella Fitzgerald really is immortal. <b>
  <i>Dream a little dream of me...</i>
</b> is apparently Constantine's theme song. <br /><br />We also have appearances from the Golden Age Sandman, Etrigan, Mister Miracle and Martian Manhunter. Be still my beating heart. Also, I don't really know how to review comic books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13084749571593470120" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7582188988201290531"><b>Ok, this is cool.</b> <i>(do people still say cool?)</i> <b>Preludes & Nocturnes</b> is the first of twelve in <b>THE SANDMAN</b> series that I have put off reading far too long.....<p>It begins June 6, 1916 in Wych Cross, England.....</p><p>In an attempt to capture and bind <b>DEATH</b> to achieve immortality, the creepy looking and evil Daemon King, Roderick Burgess mistakenly apprehends her brother <b>DREAM</b> instead.....</p><p><b>DREAM</b> <i>(Morpheus)</i> is robbed of his powers, but <u>will not talk</u>.....<b>"Confined in a glass box for three score years and ten.</b></p></span>
  <span id="freeText7582188988201290531" style="display:none"><b>Ok, this is cool.</b> <i>(do people still say cool?)</i> <b>Preludes &amp; Nocturnes</b> is the first of twelve in <b>THE SANDMAN</b> series that I have put off reading far too long.....<p>It begins June 6, 1916 in Wych Cross, England.....</p><p>In an attempt to capture and bind <b>DEATH</b> to achieve immortality, the creepy looking and evil Daemon King, Roderick Burgess mistakenly apprehends her brother <b>DREAM</b> instead.....</p><p><b>DREAM</b> <i>(Morpheus)</i> is robbed of his powers, but <u>will not talk</u>.....<b>"Confined in a glass box for three score years and ten. A human LIFETIME. TIME moves no FASTER for my kind than it does for humanity, and in PRISON it CRAWLED at a snail's pace...I was...I am...the LORD of this REALM of DREAM and NIGHTMARE."</b></p><p>The adventure really picks up speed after <b>DREAM</b> is finally freed and begins to search for his stolen tools to regain his super powers, but it wasn't until I read the last two chapters, "Sound and Fury" and "The Sound of her Wings" with <b>DREAM'S</b> sister's appearance that I was convinced I must continue on with the entire series. Goth Girl <b>DEATH</b> is so cool....and oh how she captures Franklin!</p><p>Creepy tricks and treats throughout with wonderful illustrations <b>and</b> Neil Gaiman for an author equals a great read!</p></span>

<div id="review_223941914" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/223941914" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/223941914?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781848565630" href="/book/show/7966432-preludes-nocturnes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223941914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17600239634521894362">I read this one some 10 years or so ago when I was slowly returning to comics and, having re-read it now, I still maintain that Preludes and Nocturnes is a poor place to start with this series - though it’s a decent book. <br/><br/>My first time around, I read Sandman totally out of sequence starting with Vol 3, then Vol 5, then a couple more volumes (I was just grabbing whatever was on the shelves that week!) and I read Vol 1 towards the end thinking what an unimpressive first volume it was. <br/><br/>I’d recommen</span>
  <span id="freeText17600239634521894362" style="display:none">I read this one some 10 years or so ago when I was slowly returning to comics and, having re-read it now, I still maintain that Preludes and Nocturnes is a poor place to start with this series - though it’s a decent book. <br /><br />My first time around, I read Sandman totally out of sequence starting with Vol 3, then Vol 5, then a couple more volumes (I was just grabbing whatever was on the shelves that week!) and I read Vol 1 towards the end thinking what an unimpressive first volume it was. <br /><br />I’d recommend someone looking to understand the brilliance of this series to start with the standalone books, Vol 3 and Vol 6 rather than with Vol 1 - those are much more representative of why people love Sandman so much. <br /><br />Alright - enough prelude! Onto the… nocturnes… ?<br /><br />Set in the early 20th century, an Aleister Crowley-esque type tries to summon Death and gain immortality - except he botches the spell and gets Dream instead. Dream is imprisoned for 70 years until he escapes and begins to resume his role in the universe. But first he must gather his instruments: his helm, his dreamstone, and his bag of sand. <br /><br />My biggest complaint of this book is the same criticism I have for a lot of Neil Gaiman’s work: the pacing is much too slow. But this is especially pronounced in a comic! Gaiman’s style was - and is - that of a long-winded storyteller who can spin a good yarn but will not be rushed and this can lead to a much less exciting read. <br /><br />Not only that but he comes from the Alan Moore school of writing comics where each page is severely bogged down with blocks of text. Though, unlike Moore, its more clumsy in this one and feels like Gaiman is still learning to write comics by trying to make his novelistic approach fit a different genre and not quite succeeding. <br /><br />That and Gaiman clearly hasn’t found his voice yet. The first volume is very horror-centric in sharp contrast to the rest of the series which leaves this hackneyed approach behind to successfully blend fantasy, the cosmic, and Gaiman’s own style into something unique. It’s a bit of a muddle to wade through which is why it’s a bit unfortunate that this is the first volume where a lot of people will start and which is bound to put some readers off from sampling the rest of this excellent series. <br /> <br />You can also see Gaiman relying a bit too much on the DC Universe in this first volume whereas later - though some DCU characters and locations crop up now and then - Sandman’s original cast becomes the central focus. Arkham Asylum puts in a cameo where we meet Jonathan Crane aka Scarecrow, and, as Dream sets about collecting his necessaries, he encounters John Constantine, Martian Manhunter, and Etrigan. <br /><br />We also meet a lot of new characters who will go on to become quite beloved by the fans: Cain and Abel, Lucien, the Hecateae, Lucifer Morningstar (who will get his own spin-off title), and, arguably the most popular character Gaiman’s ever created, Death, Dream’s sister (who also gets a couple of standalone books). <br /><br />Besides introducing characters, Gaiman’s first go at Sandman indicates the kind of series it will become. While Gaiman references earlier incarnations of the character like the 1930s Wesley Dodds Sandman (Dream’s helm looks a lot like Dodds’ gas mask), and a dream sequence drawn in a Jack Kirby style (a nod to the 1970s Kirby Sandman), Gaiman’s Sandman is decidedly more modern and set in a richly imaginative world. <br /><br />Beginning to distance itself from the DCU, Gaiman’s Sandman goes to Hell, he battles an evil magician across space and time, and, more importantly, he begins to explore the potential of dreams. This is the reason behind the series’ success: dreams. Because ANYTHING can happen in a dream meaning anything can happen in this series and you can see Gaiman beginning to realise that potential in this book. In one particular dream sequence the art switches to pure Kirby so we suddenly get a three-page Fourth World-esque strip out of nowhere! <br /><br />I know some people don’t like Sam Kieth’s art in this book and, to be honest, when I first read this I didn’t much care for it either. But I’ve since read Kieth’s The Maxx and come to a new appreciation of his style. His art in this book is more restrained than in The Maxx but he brings the fantastic when he needs to and the kind of bizarre comics he creates is a good fit for the type of comic Sandman is aiming for. Mike Dringenberg and Malcolm Jones III’s art is also very good though, going back to the horror theme, there’s far too much black on the pages. <br /><br />There’s a lot to like about this first volume: a lot of ideas and imagination swirling together, some great episodes like Dream going to Hell and meeting Lucifer, or John Dee’s 24 hour nightmare issue in the diner, and Dream and Death’s first scene together. But there’s a lot of chaff here too and it’s less like reading a comic and feels too often like reading an illustrated book. <br /><br />Sandman Vol 1 might not be a great comic but it leads into a great series and, if you weren’t that impressed after reading this and was wondering what all the fuss is about, keep going because it gets a lot better very quickly. Better yet, skip Vol 1 entirely and come back to it later! </span>
  <a data-text-id="17600239634521894362" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_753432098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/753432098" />
    <a title="Terry " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1262683-terry"><img alt="Terry " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316628758p2/1262683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/753432098?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terry " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terry " href="/user/show/1262683-terry">Terry </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer753432098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11291255265931673117">3 – 3.5 stars<br/><br/>Ok, so, <i>The Sandman</i>. Ground breaking comic series from the early days of DC’s innovative Vertigo line. One of the many comics of the era that was trying to do new (or at least different) things with the medium and even went so far as to not only NOT be primarily a superhero book, but one that had elements that hearkened back to the old days of anthology comics in addition to telling the serialized life story of the ‘hero’. I know I’m in the minority here, and I will admit that my op</span>
  <span id="freeText11291255265931673117" style="display:none">3 – 3.5 stars<br /><br />Ok, so, <i>The Sandman</i>. Ground breaking comic series from the early days of DC’s innovative Vertigo line. One of the many comics of the era that was trying to do new (or at least different) things with the medium and even went so far as to not only NOT be primarily a superhero book, but one that had elements that hearkened back to the old days of anthology comics in addition to telling the serialized life story of the ‘hero’. I know I’m in the minority here, and I will admit that my opinion is based on one bad experience and a subsequent lack of desire to make further attempts, but to me <i>The Sandman</i> series of comics represents the apex of Neil Gaiman’s writing. As far as I can see it was in <i>The Sandman</i> that Gaiman not only made his name in the industry (a name that would ultimately pave his way out of comics and into not only the world of prose, but also as something of a genre celebrity), but it’s also where he developed the style, preoccupations, and motifs that would come to characterize, in one form or another, all of his later works. <br /><br />“Preludes and Nocturnes” is the first storyline of the comic, taking up the first eight issues of the monthly run. In it we have Gaiman starting with the name of a golden age superhero and creating a new character based on its mythological antecedents. Taking a cue from Zelazny’s Amber chronicles Gaiman then built up a backstory for Morpheus, the godlike king of dreams, and made him part of a fractious and dysfunctional family known as ‘the Endless’…though most of that is (with one major exception) only alluded to in hints and asides in the first story arc and will be developed more fully later in the series, ultimately becoming the lynchpin of the tale of this version of the Sandman. What Gaiman does centre on in this arc are the horror anthology roots of his story as well as the set-up for Morpheus’ quest to regain his lost place and powers. It appears that some naughty occultists of the early 20th century (think Aleister Crowley and his Golden Dawn cronies) want to capture Death and rule the world. Instead they end up trapping her younger brother Dream, with disastrous results for many people who are no longer able to sleep, dream, or hope. Unable to extort power or promises from their silent victim they instead steal from him the artifacts he carried and leave him imprisoned both physically and spiritually on the mortal plane. Of course, things can’t remain thus and through the passage of time and loosening of vigilance Morpheus is finally able to escape and wreak vengeance on the mortals that dared imprison him. The next step is a standard quest narrative as the Sandman, weakened by years of imprisonment and the loss of his artifacts (into which he had poured much of his being and power…shades of Tolkien there), travels from the mortal plane to the realm of hell in an attempt to regain his tools and place in the cosmos. Standing in his way are both the triumvirate that rules hell and its legions of demons and the demented mind of a minor supervillain whose tinkering with the vast powers of Dream could end not only one of the Endless, but the world as we know it. We also get a coda to Morpheus’ quest in which we meet his older sister Death (an ironically upbeat reaper and perennial fan-favourite for the duration of the series who spawned a few spin-offs of her own) and get a glimpse into the role this strange family plays in the cosmos.<br /><br />I like what Gaiman has done here, inserting many easter eggs and call outs to various elements of the DC universe (and the more esoteric aspects of our own), but still bringing to it his own point of view and building upon it his own mythology. Whether it’s a sly call-out to the origins of Morpheus’ golden age hero namesake, a visit to the JLI embassy to intrude upon the lives and dreams of two of its members, or the insertion of a pair of characters from an old horror anthology comic as members of Morpheus' retinue in the now shattered land of dreams, Gaiman has done his homework and incorporated it into some admirable worldbuilding. I definitely noticed that this first arc of <i>The Sandman</i> was fairly heavy in its use of horror elements with many gruesome deaths and a lot of macabre imagery (as opposed to the strong fantasy flavour that came to dominate the later story arcs). Knowing where the story is heading also made this read an interesting one, as references to characters and events that will come to loom large in the story of Morpheus are things that I certainly missed my first time around, though they now give an added depth and reality to what Gaiman has penned. Why only 3 - 3.5 stars? Probably because the more gruesome aspects of the story didn’t appeal to me as much and I know things are going to get even better. This was a good start, but Morpheus has a long, and interesting, road ahead of him. Also, even this early it’s obvious that Morpheus is a bit of a prig so I don’t always sympathize with him as the protagonist. He’s ultimately much more interesting as a vehicle for the stories of others than as the ‘hero’ of his own. Of course, given his role in the multiverse that is altogether appropriate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11291255265931673117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8CkfmUMNK+z/L/fNfuPC/wEGUyjtgX1iSZpbgDtjMacqKnkHr36RUpi0LnrDg7R5h8w2imE4UPDrwmaZL5eQYQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EttR8uSpeSD2foEibGpgO6UypB6N3xXDpXWHq6841k3I2DdsCNrDnpHlWJXRCha9I/jBvAFmOFEHLbqyu8x3iw==')})
  }
